style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cm</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;mso-fareast-language:JA">
</span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;color:black;mso-fareast-language:
JA">他</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;color:black"><span
style="mso-spacerun: yes">   </span></span><span style="font-size:
11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cn</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;mso-fareast-language:JA">
</span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;color:black;mso-fareast-language:
JA">自</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">\]</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>Stanza 32 expands (makes explicit) this experience
with interpenetration (and transcendence) of time (urgent, moment, eon)
and space dimensions (extensive, here, there, nowhere, everywhere). This
has also been explained by Fa‑tsang in *Hua‑yen Yi‑hai
Pai‑men*</span><span style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>co</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">: "Since a single moment has no
substance of its own it becomes interchangeable with the great eons.
Because the great eons have no substance they also embrace the single
moment".</span><span
style="font-size:10.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>38</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> \[</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>co</sup></span><span
style="font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;
mso-fareast-language:JA">華嚴經義海百門</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;mso-fareast-language:JA">\]</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>Non‑duality (*pu‑erh*</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cp</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">)
deserves separate comment. We find it in several stanzas (30, 31 and
36). It is also related to oneness (*i‑chung*</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cq</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">*—*</span><span
style="font-size:
14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">one kind), in stanzas 5,
6 and 7. Non‑duality (Skt. *advaya*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">,
*advaita*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">) was
the favorite principle in many schools of Indian philosophy, including
Buddhism. In Buddhism this has been exposed in various texts, mostly of
Mahayanic origin. </span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">\[</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cp</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:
Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;color:black;mso-fareast-language:JA">不二</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;mso-fareast-language:JA"><span
style="mso-spacerun: yes">   </span></span><span style="font-size:
11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cq</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;mso-fareast-language:JA">
</span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;color:black;mso-fareast-language:
JA">一種</span><span style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;
color:black;mso-fareast-language:JA">\]</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>In *Ashtasahasrika-prajnaparamita*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> (Ch.
XVI) it is said that the "suchness of the Tathagata and of all dharmas
is one suchness, non‑dual (*advaya*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">), not divided (*advaidhikara*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">)".</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>In *Abhisamayalamkara* </span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">(Ch.
VII) we find the: "momentary intuition of non‑duality". The commentary
says: "This form of momentary intuition represents the state when the
bodhisattva, having during a long period of time made it his habit to
negate the double aspect of the elements (as subjective and objective),
has this double representation completely removed".</span><span
style="font-size:
10.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>39</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>In *Gandavyuha*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, when
Sudhana reaches Maitreya, he is introduced to a dwelling place of those
who delight in emptiness and in experiencing: the interpenetration of
all the ages of the universe; the entrance (*anupravesa*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">) of one into all, and all into
one; the non‑obstruction (*anavarana*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">) of
all phenomena; the non‑duality (*advaya*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">) of
all Buddhas.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>At the climax of *Vimalikirtinirdesa
Sutra*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">,
thirty‑two bodhisattvas explain in words the principle of non‑duality,
each one setting forth the solution of a </span><span style="font-size:
14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">pair of opposites
("coming" and "going", purity and impurity, samsara and nirvana).
Finally, Manjusri states that **non‑duality can be entered only by
abstaining from words and thoughts, and the same advice is given in
*Hsin-hsin Ming***</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**, in
stanza 8**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**VIII**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">Stanza
33 extends the principle of non‑duality to large and small. On this
subject Fa‑tsang says in *Hua‑yen Huan‑yüan Kuan*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">: "When
we see, for example, the height and width of a mountain, it is mind that
manifests this largeness; there is no largeness apart (from mind). Or
when we see the utter tinynes of </span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">a
particle of matter (*guna*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">), here again it is mind that manifests
this tinyness..." </span><span
style="font-size:10.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>40</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>With regards to influences between Hua‑yen and Ch'an,
Suzuki has long ago remarked: "While scholars of the Avatamsaka school
(Hua‑yen, D.P.) were making use of the intuitions of Zen in their own
way, the Zen masters were drawn towards the philosophy of Identity and
Interpenetration, advocated by the Avatamsaka, and attempted to
incorporate it into their own discourses. (...) The influence of
Avatamsaka philosophy on Zen masters grew more and more pronounced as
time went on, and reached its climax in the tenth century after the
passing of Tsung‑mi</span><span style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cr</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, the fifth patriarch of the
Avatamsaka school in China".</span><span
style="font-size:10.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>41</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">\[</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cr</sup></span><span
style="font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;
mso-fareast-language:JA">宗密</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;
color:black;mso-fareast-language:JA">\]</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>In *Hsin‑hsin Ming*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> we can
also find traces of this syncretism, especially in the last seven
stanzas. The relationship between Hua‑yen and Ch'an has been sensed by
contemporary authors like Gimello, who remarks: "One frequently
encounters in Hua‑yen thought difficult issues which might better be
understood if only one knew their true relationship to meditative
cultivation".</span><span
style="font-size:10.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>42</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> The
same remark stands for many stanzas in the *Hsin‑hsin Ming*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">. In
our opinion, the following stanzas are especially related to meditative
cultivation: 6, 8, 10, 12; 13, 19, 21, 24, 25, 26, 28, 29, 30, 33,
35.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>In *Ta‑ch'eng Chih‑kuan Fa‑men*</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cs</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> of the T'ien‑t'ai school we
can find similar ideas on large and small as in stanza 33. "The mind,
being single, has neither largeness nor smallness. The hair‑pore and the
city both embody the single total mind as their substance. From this we
should realize that the hair‑pore and the city are integrated in
substance and everywhere the same. For this reason the small admits of
the large; thus there is nothing large that is not small. The large
integrates the small: thus there is nothing small that is not large.
Because there is nothing small that is not large, the large may enter
the small, yet is not diminished. Because there is nothing large that is
not small, the small may contain the large, yet is not
increased".</span><span
style="font-size:10.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>43</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">\[</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cs</sup></span><span
style="font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;
mso-fareast-language:JA">大<span
style="color:black">乘止</span>觀法門</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;color:black;mso-fareast-language:JA">\]</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>However, the idea of relativity of small and large
has been introduced to the context of Chinese philosophy some thousand
years before, by Chuang‑tzu</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>ct</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> and
Hui‑shih</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cu</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">.
Chuang‑tzu (in ch. XVII, "Autumn Floods"</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cv</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">) observes that "From the point
of view of differences, if we regard a thing as big because there is a
bigness to it, then among all the ten thousand things there are none
that are not big. If we regard a thing as small because there is a
certain smallness to it, then among the ten thousand things there are
none that are not small".</span><span
style="font-size:10.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>44</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">\[</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>ct</sup></span><span
style="font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;
mso-fareast-language:JA">莊子</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;
mso-fareast-language:JA"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes">  
</span></span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cu</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:
Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;mso-fareast-language:JA">惠施</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;mso-fareast-language:JA"><span
style="mso-spacerun: yes">   </span></span><span style="font-size:
11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cv</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:
Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;mso-fareast-language:JA">秋水</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;color:black;mso-fareast-language:JA">\]</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>The other concept that connects *Hsin‑hsin
Ming*</span><span style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> and Chuang‑tzu is equality
(*t'ung*</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cw</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">).
Chuang‑tzu speaks of equality of things in ch. II: "Whether you point to
a little stalk or a great pillar, a leper or the beautiful Hsi‑shih,
things ribald and shady, or things grotesque and strange, the Way makes
them all into one. Their dividedness is their completeness, their
completeness is their impairment. No thing is either complete or
impaired, but all are made into one again".</span><span
style="font-size:10.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>45</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> And
then he adds: "There is nothing in the world bigger than the tip of an
autumn hair, and Mount T'ai is tiny. No one has lived longer than a dead
child, and P'eng‑tsu died young".</span><span
style="font-size:10.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>46</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">\[</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cw</sup></span><span
style="font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;
color:black;mso-fareast-language:JA">同</span><span style="font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;mso-fareast-language:JA">\]</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>Nevertheless, we should note the difference between
Chuang‑tzu and *Hsin‑hsin Ming*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">. For
Chuang‑tzu everything is equal, because: (a) *tao*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> is the equalizer of
everything, and (b) everything is appropriate in relation to its kind,
environment and context. In *Hsin‑hsin Ming*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
everything is equal because of emptiness and suchness.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>In stanzas 34 and *35 Hsin‑hsin Ming* </span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">exposes
the interpenetration of being (*yu*</span><span style="font-size:11.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cx</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">) and non‑being
(*wu*</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cy</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">),</span><span
style="font-size:10.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>47</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> of one
(*i*</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cx</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">) and
all (*i‑chien*</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cz</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">).
</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">\[</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cx</sup></span><span
style="font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;
mso-fareast-language:JA">一</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;
color:black;mso-fareast-language:JA"><span style="mso-spacerun:
yes">   </span></span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cy</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:
Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;mso-fareast-language:JA">有</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;color:black;mso-fareast-language:JA"><span
style="mso-spacerun: yes">   </span></span><span style="font-size:
11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>cz</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:
Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;mso-fareast-language:JA">無</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;color:black;mso-fareast-language:JA"><span
style="mso-spacerun: yes">   </span></span><span style="font-size:
11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>da</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:
Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;mso-fareast-language:JA">一切</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;mso-fareast-language:JA">\]</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>With stanza 36 the discourse is brought to the end,
because the subject is pronounced as beyond time (past, present, or
future </span><span lang="JA" style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;color:black;mso-fareast-language:JA">去來今</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">).</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**Notes**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**1.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
*Leng‑chia Shih-tzu Chi*</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>db</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> is one
of the Tun Huang</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>dc</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
manuscripts (Pelliot 3436, and Stein 2054). It was discovered in 1926,
and later included in *Taisho*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, 85.
1283-1290. Seizan Yanagida has published a critically edited version
with a Japanese translation in *Shoki no Zenshi I*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, *Zen no Goroku*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, 2 (Tokyo, 1971) pp. 49‑326.
</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">\[</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>db</sup></span><span
style="font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;
mso-fareast-language:JA">楞伽師資記</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;color:black;mso-fareast-language:JA"><span
style="mso-spacerun: yes">   </span></span><span style="font-size:
11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>dc</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:
Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;mso-fareast-language:JA">敦煌</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;mso-fareast-language:JA">\]</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**2.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> H. Ui,
*Zenshushi Kenkyu*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, I
(Tokyo, 1939), p. 71.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**3.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> Keiji
Nishitani and Seizan Yanagida, *Zenke Goroku*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, II (Tokyo: Chikuma Shobo,
1974), pp. 105‑112.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**4.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> David
W. Chappell, "The Teachings of the Fourth Ch'an Patriarch Tao-hsin
(580‑651)", in *Early Ch'an in China and Tibet*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> ed. by W. Lai and L.R
Lancaster (Berkeley: Berkeley Buddhist Studies Series, 1983), p.
89.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**5.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
Heinrich Dumoulin, *A History of Zen Buddhism*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> (London: Faber and Faber, 1963). p.
76.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**6.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
Wing‑tsit Chan, *A Source Book in Chinese Philosophy*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> (Princeton: Princeton
University Press, 1972), p. XI.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**7.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
William W. Soothill and Lewis Hodous, *A Dictionary of Chinese Buddhist
Terms*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
(Delhi: M. Banarsidass, 1977). First edition: London, 1937.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**8.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> To our
knowledge, there already exist five translations of the *Hsin‑hsin
Ming*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> in
English. The first translator, D.T. Suzuki, has published two versions
of his translation—one in D.T. Suzuki, *Essays in Zen
Buddhism*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, First
series (London: Rider, 1970), pp. 196‑201, and the other in *Buddhist
Scriptures*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">,
trans. and ed. by Edward Conze (Harmondsworth: Penguin, 1969), pp.
171‑175. His first translation was published in 1949. The second
translation was done by A. Waley, in *Buddhist Texts Through the
Ages*</span><span style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> (Oxford: Bruno Cassirer,
1954), pp. 295‑8. The third is by R. H. Blyth, *Zen and Zen
Classics*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, Vol.
I, (Tokyo: The Hokuseido Press, 1960), pp. 5\~99. The fourth is by Lu
K'uan Yu, *Practical Buddhism*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
(London: Rider, 1971), pp. 34‑8. The fifth, anonymous translation, can
be found in a manual, *Daily Chants*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> (Rochester: Zen Center,
1985).</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>At first it seemed that we could use one of these as
the basis for a new analysis of the poem, but after closer scrutiny it
was obvious that none of the existing translations were adequate for the
purpose. The fifth translation is a rather free rendering made for
immediate purposes in a Zen Center. Of the other four, some are
inconsistent in translating Buddhist technical terms (translations by
Blyth and Yu, while Suzuki's translation occasionally introduces terms
which seem to be part of a technical vocabulary (Absolute Reason), but
actually belong to Hegelian philosophy, rather than Chinese Buddhism.
Waley's translation is faithful except in technical terms. Perhaps he
lacked the knowledge of Buddhist tradition and therefore translated
technical terms as quasi‑technical (in stanza 19, *True
Perception*</span><span style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, instead of *perfect
awakeness*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">; in
stanza 21, *Wisdom*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
instead of *awakening*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">).</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**9.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
*Buddhist Scriptures*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, pp.
171‑5.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**10.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
*Essays in Zen Buddhism*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, r, p.
197.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**11.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> K N.
Jayatilleke, *Early Buddhist Theory of Knowledge*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> (London: Allen and Unwin,
1963), p. 389.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**12.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> *Zen
Dawn, Early Texts from Tun Huang*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">;
trans. by J.C. Cleary (Boston and London: Shambhala, 1986), p.
81.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**13.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> Blyth,
*Zen*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, 1, p.
53.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**14.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
*Ch'u*</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>dd</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> is a
technical term for grasping, clinging or attachment; which is understood
as a more intensive form of thirst, or craving (Skt. *tanha*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">). </span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">\[</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>dd</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:
Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;mso-fareast-language:JA">取</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;mso-fareast-language:JA">\]</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**15.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> Po
Shan, in Garma C.C. Chang, *The Practice of Zen*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> (New York: Harper and Row,
1970), p. 95.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**16.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> Jan
Yun‑hua, "Seng‑ch'ou Method of Dhyana", in *Early Ch'an in China and
Tibet*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, Lai
and Lancaster, eds. p.57.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**17.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> D.T.
Suzuki, *Outlines of Mahayana Buddhism*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> (New
York: Schoken, 1970), p. 70.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**18.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> *The
Surangama Sutra*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">,
trans. by Lu K'uan Yu (London: Rider, 1969), p. 54 and 125.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**19.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> Chan,
*A Source Book*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, p.
403.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**20.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
*Ta‑ch'eng ch'i‑hsin*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">,
*Awakening of Faith in the Mahayana*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">,
trans. by D.T. Suzuki, "Chicago: Open Court, 1900).</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**21.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> In the
second line of stanza 17 we also find the character *chu*</span><span
style="font-size:
14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, which is a technical
term for stages (Skt. *bhumi*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">) on
the bodhisattva path. If we read it in this sense it would mean that the
essence is not related to stages, and that is in accordance with the
concept of awakening which refutes stages. However, since *Hsin‑hsin
Ming*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> gives
no special attention to sudden awakening, we have chosen a colloquial
reading to abide, dwell. Blyth (*Zen*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> p. 79)
has misunderstood the second line of stanza 17, translating
*t'i*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> as
activity.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**22.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> One
vehicle has an interesting history in Indian Mahayana, which has been
lately exposed by D.S. Ruegg in "The *gotra*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, *ekayana*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> and
*tathagatagarbha*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
theories of the Prajnaparamita according to Dharmamitra and
Abhyakaragupta", and A. Kunst in "Some Aspects of the Ekayana" – both
papers published in *Prajnaparamita and Related Systems*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, ed.
by L. Lancaster (Berkeley Buddhist Studies Series), 1977.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>In various Mahayana texts the subject of One vehicle
is interpreted differently. In *Sri‑Mala*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> (*The
Lion's Roar of Queen Srimala*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">),
trans. by Wayman, A. and H., (New York: Columbia University Press,
1974), and the *Lotus Sutra*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">,
Ekayana is identified with Mahayana as a vehicle (*yana*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">) that
incorporates all vehicles. It also takes *tathagatagarbha*</span><span
style="font-size:
14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> as an explanation for
the thesis of One vehicle: an embryo of the Tathagata is present in
every sentient being, and (potentially) they are all Buddhas, which
means that *tathagatagarbha*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> is
</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">the
basis of only one vehicle—the vehicle of the *tathagatas*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">.
Finally, *samyaksambodhi*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> is
one, not various, or different, in relation to various vehicles –
yana-s.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>The gist of the interpretation in various sutras or
their commentaries is that the three *yanas*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">,
pertaining to *sravakas*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">,
*pratyekabuddhas*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> and
*bodhisattvas*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> is
</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">fundamentally
Eka‑yana pertaining to Buddhahood. The basis for this is that
all‑aspiring Buddhists are of one *gotra*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> (lineage), and all have
*tathagatagarbha*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
(embryo of *tathagatha*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">). "No
system postulating (different) vehicles indeed exists (in a certain
meaning): I teach that the vehicle is one (ultimately). \[But\] in order
to attract the childish I speak of different vehicles" (*Lankavatara
Sutra*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, cf.
Ruegg, in Lancaster, *Prajnaparamita*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, p. 295). </span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>One vehicle doctrine in the context of Japanese
Buddhist thought was reviewed by M. Kiyota, "The presupposition to the
understanding of Japanese Buddhist thought", *Monumenta
Nipponica*</span><span style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> vol. X\~11, no. 34 pp. 251‑9,
1967.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**23.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> Fung
Yu‑Lan, A *History of Chinese Philosophy*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, 11
(Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1983), p. 347; Chan, *A Source
Book in Chinese Philosophy*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, p. 410; Chang, *The Practice of
Zen*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, p.
227.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**24.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
*Surangama*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, p.
117.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**25.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> For
John R McRae, *Hsin Ming*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> is
falsely attributed to Fa-jung. See his article, "The Ox‑head School of
Chinese Ch'an Buddhism", eds., R. N. Gimello and P.N. Gregory, *Studies
in Ch'an and Hua‑yen*</span><span style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> (Honolulu: University of Hawaii Press,
1986), p. 208. On the other hand, Henrik H. Sorensen, commenting on the
authorship and contents of the *Hsin Ming*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, says:
"All in all, we must conclude that there are a number of important
points such as style, and contents which clearly allow us to associate
the text with Fa-jung and the Niu‑t'ou School... Interestingly, the
'Hsin‑hsin Ming'... has many points in common with the 'Hsin‑ming', both
as regards contents and style", H.H. Sorensen, "The 'Hsin‑ming'
attributed to Niu‑t'ou Fa-jung", *Journal of Chinese
Philosophy*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, 13:
105 (1986).</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**26.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
Sorensen, "The 'Hsin‑ming' attributed to Niu‑t'ou Fa-jung", p.
106.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**27.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> We
know that Indian Buddhism has elaborated a broad spectrum of ideas on
these matters. We find altogether some six Sanskrit terms related to
this subject.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">(a)<span
style="font:7.0pt &quot;Times New Roman&quot;">  </span></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">*Bodhi‑citta*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
designates the cognition of the necessity to step on the path
(*marga*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">) and
the decision/will to tread it. It is the power needed to tread the path,
whether it is defined in terms of an *arhat*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> or a
*bodhisattva*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">.
However, these are supposed to differ—the first one is bent on attaining
an awakening for himself, while the other is supposed to attain it in
order to lead others to the path, and awakening.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">(b)<span
style="font:7.0pt &quot;Times New Roman&quot;">  </span></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">*Bodhi*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> is
</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">awakening.
It means the full understanding (what was previously an aspiration) of
the Buddhist truths (whether in Theravada, Mahayana or Tantrayana
tradition), inner transformation of cognitive, emotional and volitional
faculties, and a transition to unconditioned (not bound by karma)
existence. It is sometimes described as "reaching the other shore", or
"turning about in the deepest seat of consciousness", or "breaking
through" the bondage of ignorance and karma. *Bodhi*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> is derived from the root
*budh*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, which
means to awake; therefore it is correct to translate it as awakening,
and less correct as enlightenment. However, light is related with
awakening in some sutras and tantras, principally in two forms. First is
the light of the all‑powerful! Buddha, which enlightens and awakes
sentient beings. Second is the individual, inner light of the mind.
Under delusion it appears as false thinking, but after awakening it
turns into the true light, radiant wisdom.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">(c)<span
style="font:7.0pt &quot;Times New Roman&quot;">  </span></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">*Sambodhi*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
(supposedly) means complete awakening.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">(d)<span
style="font:7.0pt &quot;Times New Roman&quot;">  </span></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">*Samyak‑sambodhi*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> is
"right complete awakening".</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">(e)<span
style="font:7.0pt &quot;Times New Roman&quot;">  </span></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">*Anuttara‑samyak‑sambodhi*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> is
"ultimate right complete awakening".</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>However, these terms were not used consistently. For
example, (b) and (c) were sometimes used as synonyms, as well as (d) and
(e). Perhaps we can better understand this from the point of actual
practice of meditation. There existed a practical need for terms which
would designate experiences of various quality. Besides, it was supposed
that the awakening of a Buddha is somewhat different in relation to the
awakening of aspirants (Arahants, Bodhisattvas). For example,
*samyak‑sambodhi*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> (Pali,
*samma‑sambodhi*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">) was
in Theravada a designation for the awakening of a Buddha; later it was
*anuttara‑samyak‑sambodhi*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">. In
Mahayana there was a tendency to use different terms for awakening of a
Sravaka, Pratyekabuddha, Bodhisattva and Buddha, in order to underline
the supposed difference in kind and quality.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>However, divisions of the path, meditation, insight
and of awakening, provoked a reaction, which derived its reasons partly
from theory, and partly from practice. From the theoretical standpoint
it was considered that awakening – after all – must be "in one piece",
apart from the preceding "not yet complete" forms; otherwise, it would
fall under ordinary undertakings, which are conditioned, relative, and a
matter of accumulation. From the practical point it seemed that the
practicing Buddhist is lost in a complex maze of an endless accumulation
of merits, insights, wisdoms, samadhis and awakenings. One could expect
a reaction to this in order to put things back in pristine simplicity
and proclaim that there is, after all, an only One vehicle
(*Eka‑yana*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">), one
germ of the thus‑come (*tathagata‑garbha*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">), one
nirvana, and one awakening, which is spontaneous, instant and sudden. As
*Lankavatara*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> puts
it: "It is reached suddenly and intuitively as the 'turning about' in
the deepest seat of consciousness; it neither enters, nor goes out—it is
like moon seen in water".</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><span
style="mso-tab-count:
1">         </span>Thus, sudden awakening, that caused so much
turbulence in Ch'an, was already at stake in Indian Buddhism (see: L.O.
Gomez, "Indian Materials on the Doctrine of Sudden Enlightenment," in
Lai and Lancaster, *Early Ch'an in China and Tibet*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">). The
Sanskrit term, introduced in *Abhisamayalamkara*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, was
*eka‑ksana‑abhisambodha*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> –
"complete‑awakening‑in‑one‑moment", a final removal of even the subtlest
defilement and ignorance, attained in a thunderbolt‑like
(*vajropama*</span><span style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">) samadhi (E. Obermiller, "The
Doctrine of Prajnaparamita as Exposed in the
*Abhisamayalamkara*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> of
Maitreya", *Acta*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
*Orientalia*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, XI,
1933, p. 44)**.** </span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">This
momentary intuition is said to be the end of the bodhisattva path. It is
an intuition of ultimate non‑duality (*advaya*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">). It is supposed to be the end
of a progressive (gradual) process of intuition
(*anupurva‑abhisamaya*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">). In
such a context **"gradualness" and "suddenness" were not concurrent, but
compatible parts of the same (and one) process**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">. The final realization is a matter of
moment, but this moment and suddenness have to be prepared through a
gradual building up. This can be seen even in Ch'an of the Southern
school. Sometimes, decades of training were necessary for "sudden
attainment", and integrating *t'i*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
(essence) with *yung*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
(function) – which followed "sudden attainment" in everyday life and
experience – was (for the most part) a gradual process.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**28.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> That
is one of the reasons for Fung Yu‑Lan to say: "Ideologically speaking,
the origin of the Ch'an school goes back to Tao‑sheng" – A *History of
Chinese Philosophy*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, II,
388.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**29.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
Compare the translation of this passage from *Pieh Tsung
Lun*</span><span style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> in Walter Liebenthal, *The
Book of Chao*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> –
</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">Peking:
The Catholic University 1948 p. 187; also, Fung Yu‑Lan, A *History of
Chinese Philosophy*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, II,
p. 278.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**30.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> L.O.
Gomez, "Indian Materials on the Doctrine of Sudden Enlightenment", pp.
393-405.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**31.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> This
character (*ming*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">) has a
long history in Chinese philosophy. It was introduced back at the time
of Lao tzu: "All things, howsoever they flourish, return to their root.
This return to the root is called quiescence, which is called the
invariable. To know this invariable is called enlightenment
(*ming*</span><span style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">)" – *Tao Te Ching*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">,
XVI.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**32.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> Chang,
*The Practice of Zen*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, p.
162‑3.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**33.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> See
Fung Yu‑Lan, A *History of Chinese Philosophy*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, II, pp. 337, 356 and 381. In
time, Chinese Buddhism developed the whole specter of technical
equivalents for Sanskrit terms (either in meaning, or as
transliterations). For example, for *bodhi,* </span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">beside
*wu,*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> and
*chüeh,* </span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">we find
a transliteration *p'u-t'i*</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>de</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">. For *sambodhi,* </span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">beside
*cheng‑chüeh*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, we
find a transliteration *san-p'u-t'i*</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>df</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">. For *samyak‑sambodhi*
</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">we find
*teng cheng‑chüeh*</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>dg</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, and
for *anuttara‑samyak‑sambodhi,*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> there
is *cheng-teng cheng‑chüeh*</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>dh</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">. </span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">\[</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>de</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:
Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;mso-fareast-language:JA">菩提</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;color:black;mso-fareast-language:JA"><span
style="mso-spacerun: yes">   </span></span><span style="font-size:
11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>df</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:
Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;mso-fareast-language:JA">三菩提</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;color:black;mso-fareast-language:JA"><span
style="mso-spacerun: yes">   </span></span><span style="font-size:
11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>dg</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:
Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;mso-fareast-language:JA">等正覺</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;mso-fareast-language:JA"><span
style="mso-spacerun: yes">   </span></span><span style="font-size:
11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>dh</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:
Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;mso-fareast-language:JA">正等正覺</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;mso-fareast-language:JA">\]</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**34.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> *The
Book of Chao*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, p.
109.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**35.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> Fung
yu‑Lan, A *History of Chinese Philosophy*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, II,
p. 331.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**36.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> Ibid.
p. 361.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**37.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
Equality, or sameness (*t'ung*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> – Skt.
*samata*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">), of
all things is one of the favorite subjects in *Hsin‑hsin
Ming*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">. Some
authors observed that equality of things was attained in Indian Buddhism
primarily by reducing all things to the common level of insignificance,
and in Hua‑yen by raising all things to the common level of supreme
value. We cannot say that *Hsin‑hsin Ming*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
applies either of these standpoints. In stanza 14 we see that dualities
are equal on the basis of emptiness, which is their common "ground". In
stanzas 30-31 equality is based on suchness and non‑duality. In stanza
33 equality appears when boundaries and limits are seen as conventions.
Thus, equality is here neither equality in insignificance, nor in
value.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**38.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> Garma
C.C. Chang, *The Buddhist Teaching of Totality*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> (London: Allen and Unwin,
1972), p. 160.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**39.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
Obermiller, "The Doctrine of Prajnaparamita as Exposed in the
*Abhisamayalamkara*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> of
Maitreya", p. 83.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**40.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> Fung
Yu‑Lan, A *History of Chinese Philosophy*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, II,
p. 348.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**41.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
Suzuki, *Essays in Zen Buddhism*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, Third
Series, pp. 19‑20. </span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**42.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> R. M.
Gimmello, "Early Hua‑yen, Meditation, and Early Ch'an: Some Preliminary
Remarks", *Early Ch'an in China and Tibet*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, p.
155.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**43.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> Fung
Yu‑Lan, A *History of Chinese Philosophy*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">, II,
p. 372.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**44.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> *The
Complete Works of Chuang Tzu*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">,
trans. by Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press,
1968).</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**45.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> Ibid.
pp. 40‑1.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**46.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> Ibid.
p. 43</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**47.**</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
Compare *Hsin Ming*</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>di</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">: "If
one puts an end to the two extremes (of being and not being), then one
will be both bright and clear"</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>dj</sup></span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">
(Sorensen, "The 'Hsin‑ming'...", p. 107). </span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">\[</span><span
style="font-size:11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>di</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:
Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;mso-fareast-language:JA">心銘</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;mso-fareast-language:JA"><span
style="mso-spacerun: yes">   </span></span><span style="font-size:
11.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"><sup>dj</sup></span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;mso-fareast-language:JA">
</span><span lang="JA"
style="font-family:Osaka\2212\7B49\5E45;mso-fareast-language:JA">雙泯對治湛然明淨</span><span
style="font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">\]</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span>

<span
style="font-size:16.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">**Two
Mainstream Translations of the *Hsin-hsin Ming***</span><span
style="font-size:
16.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"></span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">Two
popular translations of the *Hsin-hsin Ming*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:
&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> are presented here. D.T. Suzuki has two
translations published in his *Essays in Zen Buddhism*</span><span
style="font-size:
14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> and *Manual of Zen
Buddhism*</span><span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;">,
respectively. The translations are presented side by side; only the
variant lines of the second translation are given, the identical lines
are omitted. The other translation is that of Richard B. Clarke.</span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span>

<span
style="font-size:14.0pt;font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;"> </span>

relative;z-index:30"><span style="left:0px;position:absolute;left:639px;
top:-12px;width:362px;height:740px"> </span></span>
